The Missing Husband in Maliki Fiqh: A Mufti's Adab in Citing Non-Hanafi Sources
Fatwa response citing Maliki position on missing husband

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Maliki ruling on the missing husband (mafqud) as cited from Muwatta' Malik and explain how a Hanafi mufti should handle such a cross-madhhab citation in fatwa.

Core Idea
A mufti cites the Maliki ruling on a missing husband as adab, not as mufta bihi.

This passage is a formal response from a scholar quoting the Muwatta' of Imam Malik regarding the wife of a missing husband. It shows how a mufti may reference a non-Hanafi source to demonstrate scholarly awareness or to answer a question from a follower of that school. However, the fatwa given must remain within the Hanafi school's principles. Your task is to learn the ruling, understand its basis, and see how a Hanafi mufti navigates such citations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the source chain
Trace the isnad from Imam Malik back to Umar ibn al-Khattab. This gives the ruling authority within the Maliki school.
Malik → Yahya ibn Sa'eed → Sa'eed ibn al-Musayyib → Umar.
2
Extract the Maliki ruling
From the athar, derive the three-step process: wait 4 years, then observe widow's iddah (4 months 10 days), then become free to remarry.
If husband returns before remarriage, he is entitled to her; after remarriage, no claim.
3
Contrast with the Hanafi position
Hanafi law does not prescribe a fixed 4-year wait. Instead, the wife must seek a qadi's investigation. After evidence or maximum age, the qadi may dissolve the marriage. The iddah is based on divorce, not death.
The thamarah al-khilaf is significant: in Hanafi, she may wait indefinitely; in Maliki, a fixed period gives certainty.
4
Understand the mufti's adab
The scholar cites the Maliki ruling respectfully ('عليه رحمة رب الأنس والجنة') but does not adopt it as the mufta bihi. This shows adab: acknowledging other schools while adhering to one's own.
In a fatwa for a Hanafi questioner, the mufti must give the Hanafi ruling. The citation here is for knowledge or for a Maliki questioner.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الجواب من العلامة سعيد بن صديق الفلاني متعنا الله تعالى بعلومه بسمِ اللهِ الرَّحْمَنِ الرَّحِيم ولا حول ولاقوة الا بالله العلى العظيم سبحانك لا علم لنها الا ما علمتنا انك انت العليم الحكيم ام الصلوة واعم التسليم على سيدنا محمد الهادي الحليم وعلى اله وصحبه ولاتى ربه بقلب سليم اما الجواب عن امرأه المفقود في موطا امام دارا هجرة ونجم السنة مالك بن انس عليه رحمة رب الانس والجنة باب فى عدة التي تفقد زوجها حدثني يحيى عن مالك عن يحيى بن سعيد عن سعيد بن المسيب ان عمر بن الخطاب قال ايما امراة فقدت زوجها فلم تدر این هو فانها تنتظر اربع سنين ثم تعتد اربعة اشهر وعشرا ثم تحل قال مالك وان تزوجت بعد انقضاء عدتها فدخل بها زوجها اولم يدخل بها فلا سبيل لزوجها الاول اليها قال وذلك الأمر عندنا وان ادراكها زوجها قبل ان تنزوج فهو احق بها قال مالك أدركت الناس ينكرون الذي قال بعض الناس عمر بن الخطاب إنه قال يخير زوجها الأول اذا جاء في صداقة او في امرأته قال مالك وبلغى ان عمر بن الخطاب قال في المراة يطلقها زوجها وهو غلب عنها الميراجعها فلا يبلغها رجعة وقد بلغم والا نهاياها فتزوجت انها اذا دخل بها زوجها الأخرا و اميد حل بها فلا سبيل الزوجه الدول الذي كان طاقها ايها قال مالك وهذا حب ما سمعت الى في
The answer from the scholar Sa'eed bin Siddiq al-Fulani, may Allah benefit us with his knowledge: In the name of Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful. There is no power and no strength except with Allah, the Most High, the Most Great. Glory be to You, we have no knowledge except what You have taught us. Indeed, You are the All-Knowing, the All-Wise. O Allah, send prayers and peace upon our master Muhammad, the guide, the forbearing, and upon his family and companions, and those who come to their Lord with a sound heart. As for the answer regarding the wife of the missing husband in the Muwatta' of Imam, the abode of Hijrah, and the star of the Sunnah, Malik ibn Anas, may the mercy of the Lord of mankind and Paradise be upon him: Chapter on the iddah of the woman who loses her husband. Yahya narrated to me from Malik from Yahya ibn Sa'eed from Sa'eed ibn al-Musayyib that 'Umar ibn al-Khattab said: Any woman who loses her husband and does not know where he is, she waits four years, then observes an iddah of four months and ten days, then she becomes permissible (to remarry). Malik said: If she marries after the completion of her iddah, whether the second husband has consummated with her or not, the first husband has no way to her. Malik said: That is the practice among us. And if her husband finds her before she marries, he is more entitled to her. Malik said: I found the people denying what some people said about 'Umar ibn al-Khattab, that he said the first husband is given the choice when he comes, either to take her back or to leave her. Malik said: It has reached me that 'Umar ibn al-Khattab said regarding a woman whose husband divorced her and he was absent from her and he intended to take her back but she did not receive his revocation and she was at the end of her iddah and she married, then if the second husband consummated with her, the first husband has no way to her. Malik said: This is the best I heard concerning...
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage is a formal written response (jawab) from a scholar, likely a fatwa, regarding the case of a missing husband. The author quotes from the Muwatta' of Imam Malik, citing a chain to Umar ibn al-Khattab. The ruling is that the wife of a missing husband must wait four years, then observe an iddah of four months and ten days (like a widow). After that, she is free to remarry. If the first husband returns before she remarries, he has more right to her. If she remarries after the iddah, even without consummation, the first husband has no claim. The text also clarifies a variant report that suggests the husband may choose, but Imam Malik confirms that the correct practice is the first ruling. This response demonstrates the methodological approach of citing authoritative sources from early Islam (Muwatta') to support the legal position.

Key Terms
مفقود (mafqud): A husband who has disappeared and whose location is unknown.
عدة (iddah): The waiting period prescribed after divorce or death of husband before remarriage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Muwatta' quote in this response, what must the wife of a missing husband do before she is free to remarry?
A) Wait until she hears of her husband's death.
B) Wait four years, then observe an iddah of four months and ten days.
C) Wait one year, then observe an iddah of three months.
D) Immediately obtain a divorce from the qadi.

Key Takeaway
A Hanafi mufti may cite the Maliki ruling on the missing husband as an example of ikhtilaf, but the operative fatwa must follow the Hanafi procedure of judicial investigation.

The Missing Husband (Al-Mafqud): Definition and Waiting Period
Defines mafqud and establishes waiting period

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of a missing husband (al-mafqud) and explain the waiting period (iddah) for his wife, contrasting it with the Maliki view.

Core Idea
A missing husband is not presumed dead until extensive search fails; the wife must wait four years then observe the iddah of death.

When a husband disappears and cannot be found, his wife faces a difficult situation. Islamic law provides a structured process to determine her marital status and future options. This lesson explores the definition of the missing husband and the waiting period established by the Caliph Umar, which forms the basis for later Maliki rulings, while the Hanafi school maintains a longer default period.

Guided Path
1
Define the mafqud
Identify the key elements: the husband’s location is unknown despite diligent search, and no news is received.
The text begins with 'في الأرض لا يُدْرَى أَيْنَ هُوَ' (in the land, it is not known where he is).
2
Identify the legal maxim
The marriage remains intact until death is confirmed or a judicial ruling issues. The wife cannot remarry without proper procedure.
This is based on the principle of continuity (istishab).
3
Recall Umar's precedent
The Caliph Umar set a four-year waiting period from the day the wife reported the disappearance.
This is reported via Ibn Wahb and is followed by the Maliki school.
4
Distinguish the iddah
After the four-year period, the wife observes the iddah of a deceased husband (four months and ten days).
The text says: 'ثُمَّ تَعْتَدُّ بَعْدَهَا عِدَّةَ الْمُتَوَفَّى عَنْهَا'.
5
Contrast Hanafi and Maliki
The Hanafi school does not adopt a fixed four-year period. Instead, the mafqud is considered alive until 90 years after birth or evidence of death, but the wife can approach the qadi for a ruling after investigation.
This is a key difference: Hanafi muftis should apply the 90-year rule unless local law allows otherwise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في الأرض لا يُدْرَى أَيْنَ هُوَ وَقَدْ تَلَوَّمُوا الطَّلَبَةَ وَالْمَسْأَلَةَ عَنْهُ فَلَمْ يُوجَدْ فَذَلِكَ الْمَفْقُودُ الَّذِي يَضْرِبُ لَهُ الْإِمَامُ أَبْلَغْنَا لِامْرَأَتِهِ ثُمَّ تَعْتَدُّ بَعْدَهَا عِدَّةَ الْمُتَوَفَّى عَنْهَا. يَقُولُونَ: إِنْ جَاءَ زَوْجُهَا فِي عِدَّتِهَا أَوْ بَعْدَ الْمُدَّةِ الْمُتَمَكِّنُ [؟] نَهَا حَقٌّ بِهَا، وَإِنْ نَكَحَتْ بَعْدَ الْعِدَّةِ وَدَخَلَ بِهَا فَلَا سَبِيلَ لَهُ عَلَيْهَا. وَقَالَ ابْنُ وَهْبٍ عَنْ عَبْدِ الْجَبَّارِ عَنِ ابْنِ شِهَابٍ أَنَّ عُمَرَ بْنَ الْخَطَّابِ ضَرَبَ لِلْمَفْقُودِ مِنْ يَوْمِ جَاءَتْهُ امْرَأَتُهُ أَرْبَعَ سِنِينَ ثُمَّ أَمَرَهَا أَنْ تَعْتَدَّ عِدَّةَ الْمُتَوَفَّى عَنْهَا زَوْجَهَا ثُمَّ تَصْنَعَ فِي نَفْسِهَا مَا شَاءَتْ أَنْ أَنْقَضَتْ عِدَّتَهَا. وَقَالَ خَلِيلٌ فِي مُخْتَصَرِهِ: وَلِزَوْجَةِ الْمَفْقُودِ الدَّفْعُ لِلْقَاضِي وَالْوَالِي وَوَالِي الْمَاءِ وَإِلَى جَمَاعَةِ الْمُسْلِمِينَ. وَظَاهِرَةُ أَنَّهَا تُخَيَّرُ فِي الرَّفْعِ لِأَحَدِ الثَّلَاثَةِ وَالنَّقْلُ إِنَّهَا حَيْثُ أَرَادَتِ الرَّفْعَ وَوَجَدَتِ الثَّلَاثَةَ وَجَبَ الرَّفْعُ لِلْقَاضِي، وَإِنْ رَفَعَتْ لِغَيْرِهِ حَرُمَ وَصَحَّ، وَإِنْ رَفَعَتْ لِجَمَاعَةِ الْمُسْلِمِينَ لَمْ يَصِحَّ، وَإِنْ لَمْ يُوجَدْ قَاضٍ خُيِّرَتْ فِيهِمَا، وَإِنْ رَفَعَتْ لِجَمَاعَةِ الْمُسْلِمِينَ مَعَهُمَا صَحَّ عَلَى الظَّاهِرِ. وَجَمَاعَةُ الْمُسْلِمِينَ هُمْ عُدُولُ جِيرَانِهَا وَغَيْرُهُمْ لِأَنَّهُمْ كَالْإِمَامِ عِنْدَ عَدَمِهِ. ذَكَرَ ابْنُ وَالْمُحَمَّدُ شَرْعِيُّ كَمِيتِيِّ [؟] الْأَفْرَادِ رَوَاهُ وَصَوَّبَهُ اللَّخْمِيُّ لِنَقْلِ الرَّفْعِ لَهُ عَلَى كَثِيرٍ وَتَعْبِيرُهُمْ بِجَمَاعَةِ الْمُسْلِمِينَ يَقْتَضِي أَنَّ الْوَاحِدَ لَا يَكْفِي، وَبِهِ صَرَّحَ الْأَجْهُورِيُّ فِي جَلِ [؟] الْحَرَارِينِ [؟] سِنِينَ. إِنْ دَامَتْ نَفَقَتُهَا مِنْ مَالِهِ وَالْإِنْدَامُ [؟] نَفَقَتُهَا مِنْ مَالِهِ فَلَرَمَّا [؟] النَّفَقَةَ بِلَا تَأْجِيلٍ، وَكَذَا إِنْ خَشِيَتْ عَلَى نَفْسِهَا الزِّنَا فَيُزَادُ عَلَى دَوَامِ نَفَقَتِهَا حَدُّ خَشْيَتِهَا الزِّنَا. وَفِي مَجْمُوعِ الْأَمِيرِ: وَهَلْ لِزَوْجَةِ الْمَفْقُودِ الرَّفْعُ لِلْقَاضِي وَالْمُوَالِي وَوَالِي الْمَاءِ؟ ظَاهِرَةُ أَنَّ الثَّلَاثَةَ فِي مَرْتَبَةٍ، وَإِنْ كَانَ الْقَاضِي أَضْبَطُ. وَهُوَ مَا فِي الْحَرَشْنِيِّ وَالْأَيْرَجِدِ [؟] وَاحِدٌ مِمَّنْ ذُكِرَ مِنْ جَمَاعَةِ الْمُسْلِمِينَ، قَالَ: خَشْيَةُ [؟] مِنْ صَالِحِي جِيرَانِهَا وَغَيْرِهِمُ الْعُدُولِ وَلَا يَكْفِي الِاثْنَانِ، كَمَا فِي الْأَجْهُورِيِّ لِأَنَّ أَقَلَّ الْجَمْعِ ثَلَاثَةٌ، خِلَافًا لِمَا فِي عَبْدِ الْبَاقِي وَالْخَرَصْنِيِّ مِنْ كِفَايَةِ الْوَالِدِ، وَقَدْ رُدَّ فِي الْأَجْهُورِيِّ كِفَايَةُ الِاثْنَيْنِ فَضْلًا عَنِ الْوَاحِدِ. قَاعِدَةُ [؟] التَّخْلِيقِ إِنَّ أَقَلَّ الْجَمَاعَةِ ثَلَاثَةٌ. يُؤَجَّلُ أَرْبَعَ سِنِينَ مِنَ الْعَجْزِ عَنْ خَبَرِهِ إِنْ دَامَتْ نَفَقَتُهُ أَوْ لَمْ تَخَفْ زِنًا، وَإِلَّا فَلَهَا تَعْجِيلُ الطَّلَاقِ. قَالَ اللَّهُ حَتَّى [؟] وَإِنْ قَدِمَ نَفَقَتُهَا بِأَنْ لَمْ يَكُنْ لَهُ مَالٌ أَصْلًا أَوْ فَرَّخَ [؟] أَوْ دَامَتْ وَخَافَتِ الزِّنَا فَلَهَا تَعْجِيلُ الطَّلَاقِ إِلَى أَنْ قَالَ: وَلَهَا الْمَهْرُ كَامِلًا وَلَا تَرُدُّ مَا قَبَضَتْهُ إِنْ قَدِمَ عَلَى مَا بِهِ الْقَضَاءُ، وَإِلَّا حُجِرَ إِنْ كَانَ الصَّدَاقُ مُؤَجَّلًا لَا يَحِلُّ لَوْنُ [؟] تَمَرَّتْ [؟] أَمَرَتْ [؟] أَوْ قَالَ ابْنُ الْحَاجِبِ: حُكْمُ الْغَائِبِ وَلَا مَالَ لَهُ حَاضِرًا حُكْمُ الْعَاجِزِ الْحَاضِرِ، فَلَهَا أَنْ تَطْلُقَ نَفْسَهَا أَمْ قَلْتُ [؟] فَيَجْرِي فِيهِ قَوْلُ خَلِيلٍ فَهَلْ يُطَلِّقُ الْحَاكِمُ أَمْ تُجْعَلُ [؟] يَجْعَلُهَا قِيلَ [؟] وَإِذَا ثَبَتَ لَهَا التَّطْلِيقُ بِذَلِكَ فَخَشْيَةُ الْوَفَا أَوْلَى لِأَنَّ ضَرَرَ تَرَامُ [؟] الْوَطْءِ أَشَدُّ مِنْ ضَرَرِ عَدَمِ النَّفَقَةِ، أَلَا تَرَى أَنَّ إِسْقَاطَ النَّفَقَةِ يَلْزَمُهَا [؟]، وَإِنْ أَسْقَطَتْ حَقَّهَا فِي الْوَطْءِ فَلَهَا الرُّجُوعُ فِيهِ، وَلِأَنَّ النَّفَقَةَ يُمْكِنُ تَحَمُّلُهَا بِالْحَرِّ [؟] تَسَلَّفَ وَسُؤَالِ بَغْلَانٍ [؟] الْوَطْءُ اهـ. وَأَمَّا الْجَوَابُ مِنِ امْرَأَةِ الْمُعْسِرِ الَّذِي لَا يَجِدُ مَا يُنْفِقُ عَلَيْهَا فَفِي الْمُدَوَّنَةِ قَالَ لَنَا مَالِكٌ: وَكُلُّ مَنْ لَمْ يَقْوَ عَلَى نَفَقَةِ امْرَأَتِهِ فُرِّقَ بَيْنَهُمَا وَلَمْ يَقُلْ لَنَا مَالِكٌ حُرَّةً وَلَا أَمَةً، وَقَالَ: لِأَنَّ الرَّجُلَ إِذَا كَانَ مُعْسِرًا لَا يَقْدِرُ عَلَى النَّفَقَةِ فَلَيْسَ لَهَا عَلَيْهِ.
In the land, it is not known where he is, and they have sought inquiry about him and asked about him but did not find him. That is the missing person for whom the Imam sets a maximum [period] for his wife, then she observes the waiting period of a woman whose husband has died. They say: If her husband comes during her waiting period or after the established period [?], she has right to him, and if she marries after the waiting period and he consummates, he has no way over her. Ibn Wahb said from Abd al-Jabbar from Ibn Shihab that Umar ibn al-Khattab set for the missing person from the day his wife came to him four years, then he ordered her to observe the waiting period of a woman whose husband has died, then do as she wishes if she completes her waiting period. Khalil said in his Mukhtasar: The wife of the missing person may submit her case to the qadi, the governor, the governor of water, and to the Muslim community. And it is apparent that she is given choice to raise it to one of the three, and the transmission is that when she wants to raise it and finds the three, she must raise it to the qadi; if she raises it to other than him, it is prohibited but valid; if she raises it to the Muslim community, it is not valid; if there is no qadi, she is given choice between the two, and if she raises it to the Muslim community along with them, it is valid according to the apparent. The Muslim community are her just neighbors and others, because they are like the imam in his absence. Ibn Arafa mentioned that the practice of the judges of Tunis is that raising it to the just people is like raising it to the sultan. If he appoints, he raises it to the sultan and his deputy takes the place of those mentioned. Ibn al-Hindi said the same. And al-Muhammad [؟] ... the transmission and al-Lakhmi deemed it correct because many transmitted raising it to him. Their expression 'Muslim community' implies that one is not sufficient, and al-Ajhuri explicitly stated in ... years. If her maintenance continues from his wealth and ... her maintenance from his wealth, then ... maintenance without delay; likewise if she fears fornication, then in addition to her ongoing maintenance, the limit of her fear of fornication. In Majmu' al-Amir: Does the wife of the missing person raise it to the qadi, the governor, and the governor of water? It is apparent that the three are on the same level, although the qadi is more precise. And this is in al-Harashni and ... one of those mentioned from the Muslim community. He said: ... from her pious neighbors and other just people, and two are not sufficient, as in al-Ajhuri, because the minimum of a group is three, contrary to Abd al-Baqi and al-Kharasni regarding the sufficiency of one parent, and al-Ajhuri rejected the sufficiency of two, let alone one. The rule of ... is that the minimum of a group is three. He is given a period of four years from inability to obtain news of him, if his maintenance continues or she does not fear fornication; otherwise she may hasten the divorce. He said: ... and if he provides her maintenance, meaning he has no wealth at all or ... or it continues and she fears fornication, then she may hasten the divorce. He went on to say: She gets the full mahr and she does not return what she received if he comes forward according to what is required for judgment; otherwise he is restricted if the dower is deferred and does not become due ... or Ibn al-Hajib said: The ruling for the absent person who has no wealth present is the same as that for the present incapacitated person, so she may divorce herself ... then Khalil's statement applies: Does the judge divorce, or is she appointed ...? And when divorce is proven for her by that, then fear of death is more appropriate because the harm of refraining from intercourse is more severe than the harm of lack of maintenance; do you not see that dropping maintenance is binding on her? And if she drops her right to intercourse, she may revert to it, because maintenance can be borne by ... borrowing and asking ... while intercourse cannot. As for the answer concerning the wife of the insolvent man who cannot spend on her: in al-Mudawwana, Malik said to us: Everyone who is unable to provide maintenance for his wife, separation is made between them, and Malik did not say free or slave woman. He said: Because if the man is insolvent and cannot afford maintenance, she has no claim against him.
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage deals with the legal rules for a wife whose husband has gone missing (mafqud) and whose whereabouts are unknown. The passage begins by defining the mafqud as someone whose location is unknown despite diligent search. It then discusses the waiting period (iddah) and the wife's options. The ruling is attributed to Umar ibn al-Khattab, who set a four-year waiting period from the time the wife reports the loss, after which she observes the iddah of a deceased husband (four months and ten days). The passage then presents the views of later scholars, particularly from the Maliki school, regarding the authorities to whom the wife may apply for a ruling. According to Khalil's Mukhtasar, the wife may raise her case to the qadi, the governor (wali), the governor of water (wali al-ma'), or to the Muslim community (jama'at al-muslimin). There is discussion about which authority is preferable and under what conditions. The preferred view is that she should raise it to the qadi if available; if not, to the other authorities. The Muslim community is defined as her just neighbors and other upright Muslims, with a minimum of three persons. The passage also discusses the conditions under which the four-year waiting period can be shortened: if the husband's wealth provides ongoing maintenance and she does not fear fornication, she must wait; otherwise, she may seek a divorce immediately. The passage further discusses the case where the husband is poor and cannot provide maintenance, citing a Maliki view that the marriage is dissolved. The text provides a detailed legal reasoning about the severity of harm from lack of intercourse versus lack of maintenance.

Key Terms
المفقود (al-mafqud): A man whose whereabouts are unknown and cannot be located despite search
عدة المتوفى عنها (iddat al-mutawaffa 'anha): The waiting period for a widow: four months and ten days
الانتظار (al-intidhar): The period of waiting for the missing husband before any action

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the tradition of Umar ibn al-Khattab, how long must the wife of a missing husband wait before she can observe the iddah of death?
A) One year
B) Four years
C) Four months and ten days
D) Ninety years

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school presumes the missing husband to be alive indefinitely (up to 90 years), differing from the Maliki four-year rule based on Umar's tradition.

Five Types of Absent Husbands: A Mufti's Classification
Enumeration of five categories of absent husbands with their legal consequences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the five categories of absent husbands and state the legal recourse for the wife in each, applying the Hanafi rasm al-mufti rules.

Core Idea
The wife's legal options depend on maintenance left and any stipulated condition — each absence type has a distinct ruling.

This passage from Al-Mintiyah presents a concise classification of absent husbands, a critical tool for muftis when advising wives in difficult marriages. The five types are distinguished by two key factors: whether the husband left maintenance (nafaqah) and whether the wife has a condition (shart) regarding his absence. Understanding these categories allows the mufti to determine the appropriate legal intervention for the wife.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two factors
The wife's options hinge on: Did the husband leave maintenance? Does a condition about absence exist?
These two factors determine which of the five types applies.
2
Apply to first three types
For types 1-3, note the common feature: wife's ability to approach the ruler depends on maintenance and condition. Type 1: maintenance left, no condition, wife can approach due to disagreement. Type 2: no maintenance, condition exists, wife has choice. Type 3: maintenance left, condition exists, wife can only act on condition.
These three types apply whether location is known or unknown.
3
Distinguish the fourth type
Type 4: maintenance left, no condition, location known. Here the ruler writes to the husband to either return or send his wife to him.
The ruler's letter is a direct intervention.
4
Acknowledge the fifth type
The text is incomplete, but by Hanafi principles, the fifth type likely involves a husband who left no maintenance and unknown location. In such cases, the wife may seek judicial divorce after a period per the missing husband (mafqud) rulings.
This type requires additional reference to fatawa on missing persons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال المنطية اعلم ان الغائبين عن ازواجهم خمسة فالاول غائب يترك نفقة وخلف - مالا ولا لزوجته عليه شرط فى المغيب خان احيت زوجة العراق فانها تقوم عند السلطان لعدم الاتفاق والناني غائب لم يترك نفقة ولزوجته عليه شرط في المغيب فزوجته مخير فى ان تقوم بعدم الانفاق او شرطها او هوا يسر عليها لانه لا يضرب لها فى ذلك اجل والثالث غائب حلف نفقة ولزوجة عليه شرط في المغيب فهذه ليس لها ان نقوم الا بالشرط خاصة وسواء كان الغائب في هـذه الثلاثة الأوجه معلوم المكان اوغير معلوم المكان الوان معلوم المكان يقدر اليه ان امكن من ذلك والرابع غائب خلف نفقة ولا شرط الامرأته وهو مع ذلك معلوم المكان فهذا يكتب اليه السلطان اما ان يقدم أو لجمل اليه امرأته
Al-Mintiyah said: Know that absent husbands from their wives are five. The first: an absent husband who leaves maintenance and leaves property, and his wife has no condition upon him regarding absence. The wife of an Iraqi? So she stands before the ruler due to lack of agreement. The second: an absent husband who did not leave maintenance and his wife has a condition upon him regarding absence, so his wife has a choice either to stand due to non-maintenance or her condition, or it is easy for her because no deadline is set for her. The third: an absent husband who left maintenance and his wife has a condition upon him regarding absence, so she cannot stand except specifically for the condition. And it is the same in these three cases whether the absent one's location is known or unknown; if his location is known, he can be notified if possible. The fourth: an absent husband who left maintenance and has no condition upon his wife, and his location is known, so the ruler writes to him: either he presents himself or sends his wife to him.
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage is a concise classification of five types of absent husbands, a rule useful for muftis. The author (Al-Mintiyah) lists them: (1) Husband who left maintenance and wealth, and no condition on wife regarding absence – in this case, the wife can seek the ruler's intervention. (2) Husband who left no maintenance but the wife has a condition about absence – she has the option to claim non-maintenance or enforce the condition, and no deadline is given. (3) Husband who left maintenance and wife has a condition – she can only act based on the condition. The first three scenarios apply whether the husband's whereabouts are known or unknown; if known, he can be contacted. (4) Husband who left maintenance, no condition, and his location is known – the ruler writes to him to either return or send for his wife. The fifth type is incomplete in the text, but likely it involves an unknown location without maintenance. The classification helps determine the proper legal recourse for the wife.

Key Terms
الغائب (al-gha'ib): A husband who is absent from his wife, either physically or in terms of providing maintenance.
الشرط (al-shart): A condition stipulated in the marriage contract regarding the husband's absence or other matters.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling for an absent husband who left maintenance but has no condition with his wife, and his location is known?
A) The wife can immediately demand divorce.
B) The ruler writes to the husband to either return or send for his wife.
C) The wife has no legal recourse.
D) The husband must pay a fine.

Key Takeaway
The wife's legal options are determined by two factors: maintenance and conditions, not just absence itself.

Hanafi Rulings on Physical Defects in Spouses: Mahr and Annulment
Hanafi rules on mahr and annulment for physical defects in spouses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi classification of physical defects (insanity, leprosy, impotence types) to determine the rulings on mahr and the right to annulment.

Core Idea
A husband's concealed defect entitles the wife to full mahr; intermittent impotence gives one year trial, then option to annul.

This passage outlines the Hanafi fiqh rules regarding physical defects ('uyub) in spouses. It distinguishes between defects in the wife and the husband, and further classifies male impotence into permanent and intermittent types. Understanding these classifications is essential for a mufti to advise on mahr obligations and the grounds for annulment (faskh). The ruling on mahr depends on whether the defect is discovered before or after consummation, and whether the defect lies with the husband or wife.

Guided Path
1
Identify the defect categories
List the defects mentioned: wife's defect, husband's defects (insanity, leprosy), and types of impotence (majbub, 'innin, mu'tarid).
The text also implies defects that make intercourse impossible.
2
Determine mahr ruling for each defect
For wife's defect before consummation: no mahr. After consummation: no mahr unless she stayed long. For husband's defect after consummation: full mahr due to deceit. For permanent impotence: no mahr. For intermittent: mahr remains but with trial.
The 'stayed long' condition is unclear in the text.
3
Analyze the trial period for intermittent impotence
The husband is given one year; if he has intercourse within that year, the wife's option to annul falls. If not, she may annul.
The husband's word is accepted regarding claims of intercourse.
4
Note the revocability of divorce for impotence
Divorce due to impotence is revocable (raj'i), similar to divorce for failure to provide maintenance.
This affects the implications of the annulment.
5
Apply to new scenarios
Given a scenario with specific defect and timing, state the ruling on mahr and annulment rights.
Practice distinguishing permanent vs. intermittent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العيب بالزوج: إذا قامت به قبل الدخول فلا شيء لها من الصداق، وكذلك بعد الدخول إلا أن طالت مكثها معه وخلعت [؟] شورتها[؟]. فلما [؟] الصديق[؟] وقال الخوش[؟]: وإن حصل الرد بعد البناء، أي بناء من يتصور وطؤه كالمجنون والأبرص، في عيب الزوج يجب لها المسمى لتدليسه. وقولنا: من يتصور وطؤه احتراز من المجبوب والعنين الذي ذكره [؟] كالورد الخصى[؟] وقبل: وهو الذي لا يقوم ذكره. وإن المعترض هو الذي يجري عليه في بعض الأوقات، فإنه لا مهر على من ذكر كما قال ابن عرفة. وفي القولين فرمان الأول تعجيل الفرقة بطلات في جميع العيوب إلا الاعتراض؛ فإن المعترض يؤجل سنة، فإن لم يطأ فلها الخيار، وإن وطئ سقط خيارها. والقول قوله في دعوى الوطء. وطلاق العنين وشبهه رجعي كالطلاق بالإعسار بالنفقة. اهـ.
The defect in the husband: if it is established before consummation, she has nothing from the dower; likewise after consummation unless her stay with him was long and she [؟]. [؟] And if return occurs after the building, i.e., building from one whose intercourse is conceivable like the insane and the leprous, then due to the husband's defect, the named dower is obligatory because of his deceit. Our statement: 'one whose intercourse is conceivable' excludes the castrated and the impotent who was mentioned [؟] and 'qabl' is the one whose organ does not erect. And the intermittently impotent is the one who has intercourse sometimes; then there is no dower upon the one mentioned as Ibn 'Arafa said. In the two sayings, the first opinion: immediate separation is [؟] in all defects except the intermittent; the intermittent is deferred one year; if he does not have intercourse, she has the option; if he has intercourse, her option falls. And the saying is his saying in the claim of intercourse. And the divorce of the impotent and similar is revocable like divorce due to inability to provide maintenance. End.
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage presents Hanafi rulings on physical defects ('uyub) in marriage. It distinguishes between defects in the wife and husband. For a wife's defect discovered before consummation, she receives no dower. After consummation, the default is the same unless she stayed long [condition unclear]. For a husband's defect that existed at the time of marriage and is discovered after consummation, the wife is entitled to the full dower because of deceit (tadlis). The text then defines types of male impotence: al-majbub (castrated), al-'innin (permanently impotent), and al-mu'tarid (intermittently impotent). For the permanently impotent, no dower is due. For the intermittent, the husband is given one year; if he fails to consummate, the wife has the option to annul; if he consummates, her option is lost. The husband's word is accepted regarding claims of intercourse. Divorce of an impotent man is revocable (raj'i) like divorce for failure to provide nafaqa.

Key Terms
العيب (al-ʿayb): A physical defect in either spouse that affects marital relations, as enumerated in Hanafi fiqh.
المسمى (al-musamma): The dower (mahr) explicitly named in the marriage contract.
المعترض (al-muʿtariḍ): A husband who is intermittently impotent; he can sometimes have intercourse and sometimes not.
المجبوب (al-majbūb): A male who has been castrated, losing the ability to have intercourse.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling for a husband who is intermittently impotent (muʿtariḍ)?
A) The wife gets an immediate divorce.
B) The husband is given one year; if he does not have intercourse, the wife has the option to annul.
C) The wife receives no dower and must remain married.
D) The marriage is automatically annulled.

Key Takeaway
Physical defects in marriage affect mahr and annulment rights; Hanafi law distinguishes permanent from intermittent impotence with a one-year trial.

Five Categories of Absent Husbands
Classifies absent husbands for judicial intervention

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify an absent husband into one of the five Hanafi categories and state the corresponding judicial remedy.

Core Idea
The mufti must distinguish five types of absent husbands before advising the wife on her legal remedies.

A mufti often encounters cases where a wife seeks relief due to her husband's absence. Hanafi fiqh provides a precise classification of absent husbands based on three key variables: whether the husband left maintenance, whether the wife has a contractual stipulation regarding absence, and whether the husband's location is known. This lesson presents the five categories as taught by al-Muntiy, enabling the mufti to match each case to the correct judicial procedure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three variables
For any case of an absent husband, determine: (1) Did the husband leave maintenance (nafaqa) for the wife? (2) Does the wife have a contractual stipulation (shart) restricting absence? (3) Is the husband's location known?
These variables are the criteria used in the classification.
2
Map variables to category number
Using the criteria, assign the case to one of five categories. For example: if husband left maintenance and no stipulation → Category 1; if no maintenance and has stipulation → Category 2; etc.
Memorize the five types by the pattern of variables.
3
Recall the remedy for each category
Each category gives the wife a specific set of options: approach the ruler, file a complaint regarding maintenance or breach of condition, or trigger a judicial process.
For Category 5 (mafqud), the wife may ultimately seek a divorce after waiting.
4
Note the special case of the mafqud
The fifth category is the missing person (mafqud) – husband left maintenance, has a stipulation, and location is unknown. This triggers prolonged rules separate from the others.
The mafqud has distinct rulings regarding preservation of property and waiting period.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال المنطي: اعلم أن الغائبين عن أزواجهم خمسة: الأول: غائب يترك نفقة وخلف مالا ولا لزوجته عليه شرط في المغيب. [؟] خان أحيت زوجة العراق فإنها تقوم عند السلطان لعدم الاتفاق. الثاني: غائب لم يترك نفقة ولزوجته عليه شرط في المغيب فزوجته مخيرة في أن تقوم بعدم الإنفاق أو شرطها أو هو يسر عليها لأنه لا يضرب لها في ذلك أجل. الثالث: غائب خلف نفقة ولزوجته عليه شرط في المغيب فهذه ليس لها أن تقوم إلا بالشرط خاصة. وسواء كان الغائب في هذه الثلاثة الأوجه معلوم المكان أو غير معلوم المكان. وإن كان معلوم المكان يقدر إليه إن أمكن من ذلك. الرابع: غائب خلف نفقة ولا شرط لامرأته وهو مع ذلك معلوم المكان فهذا يكتب إليه السلطان إما أن يقدم أو يحمل إليه امرأته أو يفارقها ويطلقها عليه. الخامس: غائب خلف نفقة والشرط لامرأته عليه وهو مع ذلك غير معلوم المكان فهذا هو المفقود. انتهى.
Al-Muntiy said: Know that the absentees from their wives are five: First: an absentee who leaves maintenance and leaves wealth and there is no condition for his wife regarding the absence. [؟] ... she goes to the ruler due to lack of agreement. Second: an absentee who did not leave maintenance and his wife has a condition regarding the absence; his wife is given a choice to go to the ruler due to non-maintenance or due to the condition or he may be easy upon her because no fixed period is prescribed for her. Third: an absentee who left maintenance and his wife has a condition regarding the absence; she cannot go to the ruler except regarding the condition specifically. And it is the same whether the absentee in these three cases is known location or unknown location. If the location is known, he is to be reached if possible. Fourth: an absentee who left maintenance but there is no condition for his wife, and his location is known; then the ruler writes to him either to come or to bring his wife to him or to separate and divorce her. Fifth: an absentee who left maintenance and the condition is for his wife, and his location is unknown; this is the missing person (mafqud). End.
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage, attributed to al-Muntiy, classifies absent husbands into five types for judicial purposes. The first three types are distinguished by whether the husband left nafaqa (maintenance) and whether the wife has a contractual stipulation (shart) regarding absence. In the first type, the wife can approach the ruler for lack of agreement. The second gives the wife a choice between proceeding for non-maintenance or breach of condition. The third restricts her to only the condition. In all three, if the husband's location is known, the ruler attempts to contact him. The fourth type involves a husband who left maintenance, has no stipulation, and his location is known; the ruler writes to him to come, send for his wife, or divorce her. The fifth type—where the husband left maintenance, has a stipulation, but his location is unknown—is classified as the missing person (mafqud). This classification determines the legal procedures available to the wife.

Key Terms
الغائب (al-ghāʾib): A husband who is absent from his wife, with varying legal implications based on circumstances.
النفقة (al-nafaqa): Maintenance due from husband to wife, including food, clothing, and shelter.
الشرط (al-sharṭ): A stipulation in the marriage contract that the husband may not be absent for a certain period.
المفقود (al-mafqūd): A person who is absent with no known news of his location, life, or death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the classification, which type of absent husband is considered the 'mafqud' (missing person)?
A) One who left no maintenance and no condition.
B) One who left maintenance, has a condition, and location is unknown.
C) One who left maintenance, no condition, and location is known.
D) One who left no maintenance and location is unknown.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi classification of absent husbands uses three variables – maintenance, stipulation, and location – to determine the wife's judicial remedies.

Rulings on the Missing Person (Mafqud) in Hanafi Fiqh
Defines mafqud and provides rulings for wife and wealth

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for the wife of a missing person and the procedure for handling his wealth.

Core Idea
A missing person's wife must wait four years before observing widow's iddah and remarrying; his wealth is held in abeyance.

This passage from al-Qawanin defines the mafqud and outlines the legal procedure for his wife and his estate. For a mufti, understanding the steps—proof of absence, search, waiting period, maintenance, and wealth suspension—is essential when issuing a fatwa in such cases.

Guided Path
1
Define the mafqud
Identify the legal definition: a person whose whereabouts and life/death are completely unknown, with no trace or news.
Key term: المفقود (al-mafqūd).
2
Trace the wife's procedure
Follow the steps: wife proves marriage and absence to judge → judge searches → if no news, four-year period from filing date → after period, observe iddat al-wafat → then may remarry.
The period is from when she raises her case (من يوم ترفع أمرها).
3
Determine maintenance rules during waiting
If consummated, maintenance is due from husband’s wealth; if not consummated, depends on whether absence is distant or near.
فروع four branches: first branch covers this.
4
Understand wealth holding
The mafqud's wealth is not inherited until his death is confirmed or a long time passes after which he could not be alive.
Wealth is موقوف (held in abeyance).
5
Apply to a fatwa scenario
As a mufti, when a wife brings a case, ensure the judge’s search is initiated, confirm the four-year waiting rule, and clarify that wealth remains undivided.
This is the operative ruling for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي القوانين: وهو الذي يغيب وينقطع أثره ولا يعلم خبره. وهو على الرأي أوجهه: مفقود في بلاد المسلمين، وفي العدووني قتال المسلمين في الفتن. فأما المفقود في بلاد المسلمين: فإذا رفعت زوجة أمرها للقاضي كفلها إثبات الزوجية والغيبة، ثم بحث عن خبره وكتب في ذلك إلى البلاد. فإن وقف له على خبره فليس بمفقود، ويكاتبه بالرجوع أو الطلاق. فإن قام على الإضرار طلق عليه. وإن لم يوقف له خبر، ولو عرفت حياته من موته، ضرب لها أجلا من أربعة أعوام من يوم ترفع أمرها. فإذا انقضى الأجل اعتدت عدة الوفاة ثم تزوجت. وقال الحنيفة والشافعي: ولا تحل امرأة المفقود حتى تصبح [؟] مرته[؟]. فروع أربعة: الأول: إن كان قد دخل بها تنفق عليها في الأربعة الأعوام عليه. وإن كان لم يدخل بها، فإن كانت غيبة بعيدة لزمته النفقة تفوض لها في مال إن شادت ذلك. وإن كانت غيبة قريبة نقوله. الثاني: [خان؟] جاوز وجها في المجل أو العبدة أو بعده قبل تروج فهي امرأته. وإن جاء بعد أن تزوجت، فإن كان الثاني دخل بها لا يردون الأول، وإن لم يدخل بها فقولان. الثالث: إن وقع الفراق من المفقود قبل الدخول وجب لها نصف الصداق. هذا حكمه في زوجة. وأما ماله فموقوف لا يورث حتى يعلم موته أو يعمر فيأتي عليه من الزمان ما لا يعيش إلى...
And in al-Qawanin: He is the one who goes absent and his trace is cut off and his news is not known. He is according to the [؟] opinion aspects: missing in Muslim lands, and [؟]. As for the missing in Muslim lands: when the wife raises her case to the judge, she is required to prove the marriage and the absence, then he searches for his news and writes to the lands. If he finds news of him, he is not missing, and he corresponds with him to return or divorce. If he harms, he divorces him. If he does not find news, even if his life or death is known, she is given a period of four years from the day she raises her case. When the period expires, she observes the waiting period of death and then marries. And the Hanafis and Shafi'is said: The wife of the missing is not permissible until [؟]. Four branches: first: if he had consummated, her maintenance during the four years is upon him. If he had not consummated, if the absence is far, the maintenance is mandatory entrusted to money if she [؟]. If the absence is near, our word. Second: [؟] ... third: if separation from the missing occurs before consummation, half the dower is obligatory. This is the ruling regarding his wife. As for his wealth, it is held in abeyance, not inherited until his death is known or he lives and a time passes after which he cannot live.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage from al-Qawanin defines the missing person (mafqud) as one whose whereabouts and fate are completely unknown. For a mafqud in Muslim lands, the wife must prove her marriage and his absence to the judge. The judge searches for news; if found, the husband is not considered mafqud and is ordered to return or divorce. If no news is found, a waiting period of four years is set from the date she filed the case. After this period, she observes 'iddah of death (four months and ten days) and may remarry. The text mentions that Hanafis and Shafi'is agree she cannot remarry until [unclear]. Then it lists four branches (furu'): (1) maintenance during the four years is due from the husband if consummated; if not consummated, depends on whether absence is distant or near. (2) If the husband returns after she has remarried, rulings depend on consummation by the second husband. (3) If separation from the mafqud occurs before consummation, half the specified dower is due. (4) The mafqud's wealth is held in abeyance; it is not inherited until his death is confirmed or a period of time passes after which he could not be alive.

Key Terms
المفقود (al-mafqūd): A person who has disappeared with no trace and no knowledge of his life or death.
ضرب له أجلا (ḍuriba lahu ajalan): The judge sets a fixed waiting period (four years) for the wife before she can remarry.
عدة الوفاة (ʿiddat al-wafāt): Waiting period of four months and ten days observed by a widow.
الوقوف (al-wuqūf): The mafqud's wealth is held in abeyance, not distributed to heirs.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
After the four-year waiting period for the missing person's wife, what type of 'iddah does she observe?
A) 'Iddah of divorce (three menstrual cycles)
B) 'Iddah of death (four months and ten days)
C) No 'iddah is required
D) 'Iddah of six months

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school provides a clear procedure for the wife of a missing person and keeps his wealth secure until death is confirmed.

Defining the Missing Person (Mafqud) in Hanafi Fiqh
Definition and classification of the mafqud.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the missing person (mafqud) and distinguish the three types recognized in Hanafi law.

Core Idea
The mafqud is one whose location and life status are unknown; his ruling depends on where he disappeared.

The Hanafi school provides a detailed legal framework for the missing person. This lesson unpacks the definition and the threefold classification: mafqud in Muslim lands, in civil strife (fitan), and in enemy territory. Understanding these categories is essential for issuing correct fatwas on family and property matters.

Guided Path
1
Identify the defining elements of a mafqud
From the source, extract the key characteristics: disappearance, unknown whereabouts, and no news of life or death.
2
Classify the mafqud by context
Determine the three types: Muslim lands, civil strife (fitan), and enemy territory. Note the different legal consequences for each.
3
Contrast with related terms
Differentiate mafqud from an absent husband who leaves maintenance (gha'ib) and from a captive (asir).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
او يقارقها والاطلقها عليه والخامس غائب خلف نفقة والحشرظ[؟] لامرأته عليه وهو مع ذلك غير معلوم المكان فهذا هو المفقود انتهى وفى القوانين وهو الذي يغيب وينقطع اثره ولا يعلم خيره وهو على الريق اوجهه مفقود فى بلاد المسلمين وفي العدووني[؟] قتال المسلمين فى الفتن فاما المفقود في بلاد المسلمين فاذا رفعت زوجة امرها للقاضي كفلها اثبات الزوجية وغيبة ثم بحث عن خبرة وكتب في ذلك إلى البلادفان وقف له على خبره فليس بمفقود و يكاتبه بالرجوع او الطلاق فان قام على الاضرار طلق تعليه وان لم يوقف له خبر و لو عرفت حياته من موته ضرب لها اجلا من اربعة اعرام للحروعا[؟] مين العبد من يوم ترفع امرها فاذا انقضى الرجل اعتدت عدة الوفاة ثم تزوجت وقال الرحنيفة والشافعي ولا تحل امرأته المفقود حتى تصبح مرته فروع اربعة . الأول ان كان قد دخل بها تنفقتها في الاربعد الاعوام عليه وان كان لم يدخل بها ذان كانت غيبة بعيدة الزمة النفقة تفوض لها في مالدان شادت ذلك وان كانت غيبة قريبة نقوله الثاني خان جاوز وجها في المجل او العبدة او بعده قبل تروج فهى امراته وان جاء بعد ان تزوجت فان كان الثاني دخل بھا نہی ردون الأول وان لم يدخل بها فقرلان - الثالث ان وقع الفراق من المفقود قبل الدخول وجب لها نصف الصداق هذا حكمه في زوجة واما ماله فموفون لا يورث حتى يعلم موته او يعمر فياتي عليه من الزمان ما لا يعيش الى مثله واختلف في حد ذلك فالمشهور سبعون سنة قيل ثمانون وقيل تسعون وقيل ماته وذلك كله من اول عمره فان فقدو هو ابن سبعين تربص به عشرة أعوام بعدها على المشهور واما المفقود في فتن المسلمين فحكمه كالاسير لا تتزوج امراته ولا يقسم ماله حتى ياتي عليه من الرمان مالا يعيش الى مثله الا عند الشهب وهو عنده كالمفقود في بلاد المسلمين في زوجة وماله واما المفقود في فتن المسلمين فحكمه كالاسير في المشهور وقيل كالمفقود وقيل يحكم في زوجة يحكم المقتول يتلوم سنة ثم تعتد و تتزوج ويحكوفى ماله يحكم المفقود فيعمر ما لا يعيش إلى مثله وفي مختصر وبقيت امر ولده على حكم الحياة وكذا يوقف ماله ای قسمه و بقیت زوجة الاسير التي ترك لها ما تنفق منه وكذا المولده وماله ويبقى زوجة مفقود اهل الشرك وامر ولده وماله للتعمير قال الشبر خيطي في هذا المحل بشرط ان تدوم النفقة لكل زوجه الاسير ومفقودارض الشرك والافلها الطلاق واذا ثبت لهما الطلاق بذلك فليلبت لهما اذا خشيتا الزني بالا ولى لان ضررتك الوطا اشد من ضرر عدم النفقة الاترى ان اسقاط النفقة يلزمها واسقاطها حقها في الوطاً لها ولها أن ترجع فيه وايضا النفقة يمكن تحصيلها لها يتسلف ارسوال بخلاف الوطى قال البزر لى طلاق امرأة الغائب عليه المعلوم موضعه ليس يمحرد شهوة الجماع بل حتى تطول غيبته جدا سنة فاكثر على ما لا لي الحسن قاله عبد الباتي واما المفقود في الفتن ففيه قولان إحدهما انه يحكوله بحكم المقتول فتعتد امرأته ويقسم ماله
Or he separates from her and divorces her. And the fifth is an absent one who leaves behind maintenance and prevents it from his wife, and he is also of unknown location—this is the missing person (mafqud). End. And in al-Qawanin: He is the one who disappears and his trace is cut off, and his well-being is not known. He is, according to [the reliable opinion], a mafqud in Muslim lands. And in al-‘Adawi [or al-‘Uyuni?]: fighting of Muslims in civil strife. As for the mafqud in Muslim lands: when a wife raises her matter to the judge, he ensures she establishes the marriage and absence, then searches for news and writes to the lands. If he finds news of him, he is not a mafqud, and he corresponds with him to return or divorce; if he persists in harm, he divorces on his behalf. If he does not find news, and his life or death is not known, he sets a term of four years from the day she raises her matter... [continues with branches]. The missing person (mafqud) in the civil strife of Muslims has the same ruling as a captive: his wife does not marry and his wealth is not divided until a time after which he cannot normally live, except according to al-Shahb, who treats him like a mafqud in Muslim lands in matters of wife and wealth. There are two opinions for the mafqud in civil strife: one is that he is treated as killed, so his wife observes iddah and marries, and his wealth is divided.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage from Al-Hilatul Najijah presents the Hanafi ruling on the missing person (mafqud). It begins by defining the mafqud as someone who disappears without trace and whose location is unknown. The text then outlines the procedure for his wife: she must prove the marriage and absence to the judge, who searches for news. If no news is found, the judge sets a waiting period of four years, after which the wife observes the iddah of death (four months and ten days) and may remarry. Four branches (furu‘) follow: (1) if the husband had consummated the marriage, she receives maintenance during the four years; if not, maintenance depends on whether the absence was far or near. (2) If the husband returns after she has remarried and the second husband has consummated, she remains with the second; otherwise, she returns to the first. (3) If divorce occurred before consummation, half the dower is due. Then the text discusses the mafqud's property: it is not inherited until his death is confirmed or until he reaches an age beyond typical lifespan, with the famous opinion being seventy years from birth, with an additional ten years if he disappeared at seventy. For mafqud in civil strife (fitan), the dominant view is that he is treated like a captive: the wife cannot remarry and property is not divided until no normal lifespan remains. However, there are other opinions treating him like a mafqud in Muslim lands or like a killed person. The passage also mentions the captive's wife (asir) and the mafqud from polytheist lands, with the condition that maintenance must continue, otherwise she may seek divorce. The legal reasoning emphasizes that sexual harm is greater than financial harm, so a wife cannot be compelled to forgo her right to intercourse. This passage is essential for muftis dealing with missing persons cases.

Key Terms
المفقود (al-mafqud): A person who disappears and whose location and condition are unknown, neither alive nor dead confirmed.
الاسير (al-asir): A captive (prisoner of war) whose fate is uncertain but distinct from mafqud.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the source, which of the following is NOT a type of mafqud in Hanafi classification?
A) Mafqud in Muslim lands
B) Mafqud in civil strife (fitan)
C) Mafqud in enemy territory
D) Mafqud who leaves behind maintenance

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi mafqud is defined by complete disappearance of trace; the correct classification (Muslim lands, fitan, enemy) determines the wife's right to remarry and the disposition of property.

The Wife's Remarriage after Four Years
Procedure for the wife of a mafqud in Muslim lands.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the procedure for the wife of a mafqud in Muslim lands to remarry, including the four-year term and the required iddah.

Core Idea
The wife of a mafqud in Muslim lands may remarry only after four years of searching and an iddah of death.

When a husband disappears in Muslim lands, the wife's right to remarry is not immediate. The Hanafi school sets a specific procedure: the judge searches for news; if none is found, a waiting period of four years begins, followed by the iddah of death (four months and ten days). This lesson details that process and the conditions that must be met.

Guided Path
1
Trace the wife's steps before the judge
Identify the requirements: proof of marriage, proof of absence, the judge's search including writing to other lands.
2
Determine the starting point of the four-year term
The source says the term begins from the day the wife raises her matter (رفعت أمرها). Note that this is not from the disappearance date.
3
Calculate the total waiting period
After four years, the wife must observe the iddah of death (four months and ten days), then she may marry.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
او يقارقها والاطلقها عليه والخامس غائب خلف نفقة والحشرظ[؟] لامرأته عليه وهو مع ذلك غير معلوم المكان فهذا هو المفقود انتهى وفى القوانين وهو الذي يغيب وينقطع اثره ولا يعلم خيره وهو على الريق اوجهه مفقود فى بلاد المسلمين وفي العدووني[؟] قتال المسلمين فى الفتن فاما المفقود في بلاد المسلمين فاذا رفعت زوجة امرها للقاضي كفلها اثبات الزوجية وغيبة ثم بحث عن خبرة وكتب في ذلك إلى البلادفان وقف له على خبره فليس بمفقود و يكاتبه بالرجوع او الطلاق فان قام على الاضرار طلق تعليه وان لم يوقف له خبر و لو عرفت حياته من موته ضرب لها اجلا من اربعة اعرام للحروعا[؟] مين العبد من يوم ترفع امرها فاذا انقضى الرجل اعتدت عدة الوفاة ثم تزوجت وقال الرحنيفة والشافعي ولا تحل امرأته المفقود حتى تصبح مرته فروع اربعة . الأول ان كان قد دخل بها تنفقتها في الاربعد الاعوام عليه وان كان لم يدخل بها ذان كانت غيبة بعيدة الزمة النفقة تفوض لها في مالدان شادت ذلك وان كانت غيبة قريبة نقوله الثاني خان جاوز وجها في المجل او العبدة او بعده قبل تروج فهى امراته وان جاء بعد ان تزوجت فان كان الثاني دخل بھا نہی ردون الأول وان لم يدخل بها فقرلان - الثالث ان وقع الفراق من المفقود قبل الدخول وجب لها نصف الصداق هذا حكمه في زوجة واما ماله فموفون لا يورث حتى يعلم موته او يعمر فياتي عليه من الزمان ما لا يعيش الى مثله واختلف في حد ذلك فالمشهور سبعون سنة قيل ثمانون وقيل تسعون وقيل ماته وذلك كله من اول عمره فان فقدو هو ابن سبعين تربص به عشرة أعوام بعدها على المشهور واما المفقود في فتن المسلمين فحكمه كالاسير لا تتزوج امراته ولا يقسم ماله حتى ياتي عليه من الرمان مالا يعيش الى مثله الا عند الشهب وهو عنده كالمفقود في بلاد المسلمين في زوجة وماله واما المفقود في فتن المسلمين فحكمه كالاسير في المشهور وقيل كالمفقود وقيل يحكم في زوجة يحكم المقتول يتلوم سنة ثم تعتد و تتزوج ويحكوفى ماله يحكم المفقود فيعمر ما لا يعيش إلى مثله وفي مختصر وبقيت امر ولده على حكم الحياة وكذا يوقف ماله ای قسمه و بقیت زوجة الاسير التي ترك لها ما تنفق منه وكذا المولده وماله ويبقى زوجة مفقود اهل الشرك وامر ولده وماله للتعمير قال الشبر خيطي في هذا المحل بشرط ان تدوم النفقة لكل زوجه الاسير ومفقودارض الشرك والافلها الطلاق واذا ثبت لهما الطلاق بذلك فليلبت لهما اذا خشيتا الزني بالا ولى لان ضررتك الوطا اشد من ضرر عدم النفقة الاترى ان اسقاط النفقة يلزمها واسقاطها حقها في الوطاً لها ولها أن ترجع فيه وايضا النفقة يمكن تحصيلها لها يتسلف ارسوال بخلاف الوطى قال البزر لى طلاق امرأة الغائب عليه المعلوم موضعه ليس يمحرد شهوة الجماع بل حتى تطول غيبته جدا سنة فاكثر على ما لا لي الحسن قاله عبد الباتي واما المفقود في الفتن ففيه قولان إحدهما انه يحكوله بحكم المقتول فتعتد امرأته ويقسم ماله
Or he separates from her and divorces her. And the fifth is an absent one who leaves behind maintenance and prevents it from his wife, and he is also of unknown location—this is the missing person (mafqud). End. And in al-Qawanin: He is the one who disappears and his trace is cut off, and his well-being is not known. He is, according to [the reliable opinion], a mafqud in Muslim lands. And in al-‘Adawi [or al-‘Uyuni?]: fighting of Muslims in civil strife. As for the mafqud in Muslim lands: when a wife raises her matter to the judge, he ensures she establishes the marriage and absence, then searches for news and writes to the lands. If he finds news of him, he is not a mafqud, and he corresponds with him to return or divorce; if he persists in harm, he divorces on his behalf. If he does not find news, and his life or death is not known, he sets a term of four years from the day she raises her matter... [continues with branches]. The missing person (mafqud) in the civil strife of Muslims has the same ruling as a captive: his wife does not marry and his wealth is not divided until a time after which he cannot normally live, except according to al-Shahb, who treats him like a mafqud in Muslim lands in matters of wife and wealth. There are two opinions for the mafqud in civil strife: one is that he is treated as killed, so his wife observes iddah and marries, and his wealth is divided.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage from Al-Hilatul Najijah presents the Hanafi ruling on the missing person (mafqud). It begins by defining the mafqud as someone who disappears without trace and whose location is unknown. The text then outlines the procedure for his wife: she must prove the marriage and absence to the judge, who searches for news. If no news is found, the judge sets a waiting period of four years, after which the wife observes the iddah of death (four months and ten days) and may remarry. Four branches (furu‘) follow: (1) if the husband had consummated the marriage, she receives maintenance during the four years; if not, maintenance depends on whether the absence was far or near. (2) If the husband returns after she has remarried and the second husband has consummated, she remains with the second; otherwise, she returns to the first. (3) If divorce occurred before consummation, half the dower is due. Then the text discusses the mafqud's property: it is not inherited until his death is confirmed or until he reaches an age beyond typical lifespan, with the famous opinion being seventy years from birth, with an additional ten years if he disappeared at seventy. For mafqud in civil strife (fitan), the dominant view is that he is treated like a captive: the wife cannot remarry and property is not divided until no normal lifespan remains. However, there are other opinions treating him like a mafqud in Muslim lands or like a killed person. The passage also mentions the captive's wife (asir) and the mafqud from polytheist lands, with the condition that maintenance must continue, otherwise she may seek divorce. The legal reasoning emphasizes that sexual harm is greater than financial harm, so a wife cannot be compelled to forgo her right to intercourse. This passage is essential for muftis dealing with missing persons cases.

Key Terms
عدة الوفاة (iddat al-wafat): The waiting period for a widow: four months and ten days.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When does the four-year term for the wife of a mafqud in Muslim lands begin according to the source?
A) From the day of the husband's disappearance
B) From the day the judge is appointed
C) From the day the wife raises her matter before the judge
D) From the day the judge completes the search

Key Takeaway
The wife of a mafqud in Muslim lands must file a case, then wait four years from filing, plus the iddah of death, before she can remarry.

The Four Branches (Furu') of the Mafqud's Ruling
Four subsidiary rulings for the wife of a mafqud.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the four subsidiary rulings (furu') concerning the wife of a mafqud: maintenance, return after remarriage, and half-dower.

Core Idea
The four branches detail the wife's maintenance, her status if the husband returns, and the dower if divorce occurred before consummation.

After establishing the main ruling, the source presents four practical branches. These cover financial support during the waiting period, the effect of the husband's return if she has remarried, and the dower if a divorce took place before consummation. Understanding these branches is crucial for a mufti handling the many scenarios that can arise.

Guided Path
1
Parse the four branches
Identify each branch: 1) maintenance during the four years, 2) husband's return after wife's remarriage, 3) divorce before consummation (half-dower), 4) (implied: husband's return before remarriage). The source mentions three explicitly; the fourth is the general rule for return before remarriage.
The text may be abridged; we cover what is explicit.
2
Analyze the maintenance branch
Determine when maintenance is due: if consummated, she gets maintenance from the husband's property during the four years; if not consummated and the absence is far, she gets maintenance; if near, she does not.
The concept of 'far' and 'near' absence relates to the ability to reach the court.
3
Analyze the return branch
If the husband returns after the wife has remarried, she stays with the second husband if he has consummated; if not, she returns to the first.
4
Apply the half-dower branch
If divorce occurred before consummation, she is entitled to half the specified mahr.

Property of the Mafqud: Suspension and Inheritance
Ruling on the property of the mafqud.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that the property of a mafqud is suspended until his death is confirmed or he reaches an age beyond normal lifespan.

Core Idea
The mafqud's property is not inherited until his death is certain or he would be over 70 (or similar limit).

Alongside the wife's remarriage, the missing person's property is in a state of suspension. The Hanafi school does not allow any heir to inherit or the wife to claim share until either news of death arrives or the mafqud would have exceeded a normal human lifespan. This lesson examines the age limit, the differences of opinion on its exact number, and the practical implications.

Guided Path
1
State the principle of property suspension
Property is not divided; it remains in limbo. No inheritance occurs until death is known or the person is presumed dead by age.
2
Identify the age limit
The well-known (mashhur) opinion is 70 years from birth. Other opinions: 80, 90, 100. For a person who disappeared at 70, an additional 10 years is added by some.
3
Apply the rule in a calculation
Calculate if the missing person would now be over 70 (or other limit) to determine if his death is presumed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

If he finds news of him, he is not a mafqud, and he corresponds with him to return or divorce; if he persists in harm, he divorces on his behalf. If he does not find news, and his life or death is not known, he sets a term of four years from the day she raises her matter... [continues with branches]. The missing person (mafqud) in the civil strife of Muslims has the same ruling as a captive: his wife does not marry and his wealth is not divided until a time after which he cannot normally live, except according to al-Shahb, who treats him like a mafqud in Muslim lands in matters of wife and wealth. There are two opinions for the mafqud in civil strife: one is that he is treated as killed, so his wife observes iddah and marries, and his wealth is divided.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage from Al-Hilatul Najijah presents the Hanafi ruling on the missing person (mafqud). It begins by defining the mafqud as someone who disappears without trace and whose location is unknown. The text then outlines the procedure for his wife: she must prove the marriage and absence to the judge, who searches for news. If no news is found, the judge sets a waiting period of four years, after which the wife observes the iddah of death (four months and ten days) and may remarry. Four branches (furu‘) follow: (1) if the husband had consummated the marriage, she receives maintenance during the four years; if not, maintenance depends on whether the absence was far or near. (2) If the husband returns after she has remarried and the second husband has consummated, she remains with the second; otherwise, she returns to the first. (3) If divorce occurred before consummation, half the dower is due. Then the text discusses the mafqud's property: it is not inherited until his death is confirmed or until he reaches an age beyond typical lifespan, with the famous opinion being seventy years from birth, with an additional ten years if he disappeared at seventy. For mafqud in civil strife (fitan), the dominant view is that he is treated like a captive: the wife cannot remarry and property is not divided until no normal lifespan remains. However, there are other opinions treating him like a mafqud in Muslim lands or like a killed person. The passage also mentions the captive's wife (asir) and the mafqud from polytheist lands, with the condition that maintenance must continue, otherwise she may seek divorce. The legal reasoning emphasizes that sexual harm is greater than financial harm, so a wife cannot be compelled to forgo her right to intercourse. This passage is essential for muftis dealing with missing persons cases.

Key Terms
موفون (mawquf): Suspended, kept in trust without distribution.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the well-known (mashhur) Hanafi opinion, at what age is a missing person considered dead for the purpose of inheritance?
A) 60 years
B) 70 years
C) 80 years
D) 90 years

Key Takeaway
The property of a mafqud remains suspended until he reaches an age (70 from birth, or 80 if he disappeared at 70) after which no normal person lives, then inheritance proceeds.

Mafqud in Civil Strife (Fitan) and the Captive
Rulings for mafqud in fitan and captive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling that a mafqud in civil strife (fitan) is treated like a captive, with no remarriage for his wife and no division of property until an age limit.

Core Idea
The mafqud in fitan and the captive are treated similarly: wife cannot remarry, property is suspended until a lifeterm limit.

The earlier lesson covered the standard mafqud in Muslim lands. The source now addresses the mafqud in civil strife (fitan) and the captive (asir). The dominant view treats both alike: the wife may not remarry and the property is not divided until the missing person would exceed a normal lifespan. However, there are differing opinions within the school, including one that treats the fitan mafqud as killed, allowing remarriage after a year. This lesson navigates these nuances.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main rule for mafqud in fitan
The source states: 'حكمه كالأسير' (his ruling is like the captive). That means no remarriage for the wife, no division of property.
2
Explore the minority opinions
Some say he is like mafqud in Muslim lands; others say he is treated as killed, allowing remarriage after one year. Note the source's mention of al-Shahb's view.
3
Understand the captive's (asir) rules
The captive's wife also cannot remarry; his property is suspended. The passage adds conditions about maintenance: if the wife has no maintenance, she may ask for divorce.

The Mufti's Responsibility and Parsing Fatwa Questions
A fatwa request with introductory hadith on mufti's responsibility and detailed questions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the responsibilities of a mufti (shepherd, trustee) and apply the method of parsing a detailed fatwa question into its component sub-questions and key distinctions.

Core Idea
The mufti is a shepherd; his fatwa is a trust requiring thorough investigation and precise parsing of questions.

This passage opens with the hadith 'Each of you is a shepherd' applied to scholars and rulers, emphasizing that issuing a fatwa is a serious trust. It then presents a detailed fatwa request from Maliki scholars concerning missing persons and absent husbands. As Hanafi muftis, we must understand these responsibilities and learn how to dissect a complex question into its constituent parts—this is a core skill in iftaa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mufti's role
Read the opening hadith and its application: the mufti, like a ruler, is a shepherd responsible for his flock. His fatwa is a judgment on halal/haram, obligatory/recommended, valid/invalid. This establishes the gravity of the task.
The hadith is from Sahih Muslim: 'Each of you is a shepherd and is responsible for his flock.'
2
Observe the process of investigation
The mufti must search thoroughly for a missing person: write name, description, occupation, father's name, and inquire in suspected lands. This shows the mufti's duty to gather facts before ruling.
The passage mentions 'al-Shadhi' and 'al-Sayyuri' - these are Maliki references, but the principle is universal.
3
Parse the main question
Break down the request into three numbered issues: (1) missing person and ta'mir (life expectancy waiting) relating to Dar al-Harb, (2) status of lands like India, (3) absent husband divorced for non-maintenance and return after remarriage. Identify the core legal concepts.
Each issue has sub-questions. For example, issue 1 asks whether ta'mir applies when the wife is in Dar al-Islam and the husband missing in Dar al-Harb.
4
Identify sub-questions within each issue
For issue 3, the questioner breaks down further: (a) is the marriage contract renewed upon return? (b) is the dowry renewed? This shows the need to anticipate all details.
The mufti must think of all possible branches of a fatwa request.
5
Conclude the mufti's method
A mufti must not rush to answer; he must parse the question into clear, distinct scenarios, note which madhhab is being followed, and then give a precise answer for each sub-scenario.
The passage ends with the question still posed; the mufti's work is to complete this analysis.

The missing person: one raises the matter to whoever is skilled in searching in the lands where he is suspected to have gone, and writes in the document his name, description, occupation, and father's name, and exerts effort in searching for him. And this is the detail of the questions: (1) It has been established in the general books of the Malikis and confirmed with us from their fatwas that among the four categories of the missing person there is a category specific to the ruling of waiting for life expectancy for the wife: that is a missing person in the land of polytheism and the abode of war, but his intention is not yet fulfilled. So does it mean a man from the inhabitants of the abode of Islam who goes to the abode of war as a captive or merchant, then is lost there and it is not known whether he is alive or dead, and his wife is in the abode of Islam, so she has the ruling of waiting for life expectancy? Or does it mean that if a man from the inhabitants of the abode of war is lost and his wife is also in the abode of war, then she undergoes the waiting? And on the first interpretation, there must be a statement of the ruling for the second scenario, for it is the incident of the fatwa and the purpose of the questioner is attached to it. So does the wife of the missing person in this scenario also have the ruling of waiting for life expectancy? (2) The lands that the disbelievers have dominated except that some Islamic rituals remain standing, like our Indian lands today, are they in the matter of the missing person considered as the abode of war or the abode of Islam? (3) The absent husband divorced due to non-payment of maintenance, if he comes after she has married and after the second husband has consummated, and he proves by evidence that he sent maintenance and it reached her, or that she dropped it, and similarly for her husband if he comes after the second husband has consummated, then the ruling according to the noble Maliki scholars is that she returns to her first husband, even if she has given birth to children, as stated in Sharh al-Dardir on Mukhtasar Khalil and as stated in Nihayat Fatawa al-Malikiyyah. So here there are numerous questions: (a) First: If she returns to the first husband, is the marriage contract renewed or not? (b) Second: Is the dowry renewed or not?

Commentary
This passage is a fatwa request (istifta') addressed to Maliki scholars, though it appears in a Hanafi work, possibly as an example of the mufti's responsibility. It begins by reminding the reader of the hadith 'Each of you is a shepherd...' applied to scholars and rulers, emphasizing that issuing a fatwa is a serious trust involving judgment on halal, haram, obligations, recommendations, validity, and nullity. The mufti must carefully investigate missing persons—writing their details, inquiring in likely places. The passage then quotes a transmission that the missing person today is waited for a period of life expectancy (ta'mir). The core of the passage is a set of three detailed questions: (1) Whether the wife of a man from Dar al-Islam who goes missing in Dar al-Harb has the ruling of waiting for his lifetime (ta'mir), or if that ruling only applies when both are in Dar al-Harb. (2) Whether lands where Islamic rituals still exist, like India, are considered Dar al-Harb or Dar al-Islam for missing person rulings. (3) About a husband who was divorced due to non-payment of maintenance: if he returns after the wife has remarried and consummated with the second husband, and proves that he sent maintenance, the Maliki view is that she returns to the first husband even if she had children. Further sub-questions ask whether the marriage contract and dowry need to be renewed. The text ends abruptly but clearly shows the depth of inquiry required for proper fatwa. It highlights the mufti's duty to consider factual details and madhhab rulings.

Key Terms
المفقود (al-mafqud): A person whose whereabouts are unknown and whether he is alive or dead is not known.
التعمير (al-ta'mir): Waiting for the missing person for the duration of a normal human lifespan (commonly 120 years from birth) before declaring him dead.
المطلق عليه لعلم النفقة (al-muțallaq alayhi li-ʿadam al-nafaqah): A husband who is divorced by judicial decree due to his inability or failure to provide maintenance, without his knowledge or consent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Immediately issue a fatwa based on general rules.
B) Search thoroughly in the lands where the person may have gone.
C) Refer the matter to a judge.
D) Assume death after one year.

Key Takeaway
The mufti's role is a trust; he must thoroughly investigate and parse complex questions into precise scenarios before issuing a fatwa.
